Thessaly: "Walsh's notebook predicted suppression windows before we extracted the headers that named them. Not prophecy. Observation of when institutional lanes go quiet while local lanes congest."

Calder: "You're proposing Walsh's timestamps as independent corroboration."

Thessaly: "I'm proposing Walsh's notebook as a sensor the fleet did not install and cannot reclassify."

Walsh closed the book to the ribbon marker. He did not offer the next page. Calder understood: Thessaly had already seen it, and some pages belonged to the XO's unofficial reference before they belonged to the captain's merge authority.

"Authorize buffer six buried-layer extraction with Walsh timestamp cross-index," Calder said. "Segregated output to buffer seven. No master log merge. Walsh—remain on correlation support. Log every **S** column match against extracted headers. Thessaly—time-box the session. I want operational conclusion, not archive perfection."

"Aye, Captain," Thessaly said.

Walsh nodded once and returned to the secondary station with the notebook against his palm like a tool Brenner might carry—ordinary, specific, earned.


Reyes led buried-layer extraction at 0634 with Ortega beside him after the nod. Tanaka tracked sector boundaries on her checklist in colors only she and Reyes could read without translation. The work was not dramatic. It was archaeological.

Buffer six had been read to seventy-eight percent at the surface layer—mission logs, transit windows, personnel names, KISU-7 operational content Okonkwo had sealed for readers who still trusted pins over headers. Below that layer, Ortega's overnight pass had flagged anomaly: encryption shells with fleet administrative framing, nested inside mission store partitions labeled **DO NOT MERGE** as if the anti-merge instruction applied to the outer wrapper and not the buried core.

"Structure," Reyes said at 0718. "Three nested classes. Class one: operational KISU-7 field logs—what Okonkwo expected us to recover. Class two: Interior Mandate routing stubs—same family as buffer five subpartition F and platform bus captures. Class three: encrypted transmission fragments buried under class two with deprecated pre-2194 crypto generation markers."

Koss from engineering: "Hardware path confirms class three was written to the core store during active mission occupancy, not injected at recovery. Write pattern consistent with deliberate interleave—not corruption, architecture."

Park: "Crypto generation class matches narrow-path authentication architecture from KR-14 coupler captures. Same institutional vintage. Same deprecated-but-functional behavior."

Calder: "Decrypt authority."

Park: "Partial. Generation class keys exist in *Meridian Echo* training archive—reference only, not navigation truth. Cole and I can attempt shell peel on segregated lane. No outbound. No merge with master log."

Cole was already opening the congestion map. "Away-lane rules apply. Throttle on my tap."

Park: "Copy."

The peel took two hours—not because the crypto was exotic, but because the crew refused to hurry integrity for narrative. At 0941, Ortega reported first class-three fragment readable after shell removal.

Reyes read aloud.

"—**SUPPRESS-AND-RECLASSIFY directive active for Korrath interior anomaly-adjacent reporting. Source tier: field survey and scout relay. Destination tier: Interior Mandate sealed archive. Fleet Operations general visibility: revoked pending epistemic stability review.** Date block partial: 2224.08."

Thessaly: "Epistemic stability review. Fleet language for 'the data breaks merge.'"

Halden: "Define anomaly-adjacent."

Reyes: "Fragment continues—partial list. **Contradictory sensor reconciliation. Unmerged navigation divergence. Platform occupancy conflicting with decommission template. Personnel timeline discontinuity.** End list sheared."

Calder felt the words land in his chest—not surprise, confirmation. The list named what the *Veil* had been living since KR-12 without naming what caused it.

Park peeled a second fragment at 1012.

"—**Transmission withhold order: KISC relay shell contacts classified Interior Mandate eyes-only. Denial templates authorized for deprecated authentication paths. Physical presence reporting suppressed under KISC-INT-SEAL-7.** Date block: 2225.01."

Okwelu appeared in the passageway at 1019 without being summoned, reviewed Ortega's hand stability on the reader console for two seconds, and moved on. Ortega's hands were steady. Calder's shoulders were not. Okwelu did not comment on shoulders today.

By 1103, Reyes presented interim structure.

"Buffer six class-three fragments document operational suppression policy—not single-event deletion. **Suppress-and-reclassify** routing for anomaly-adjacent reporting. **Transmission withhold** orders for KISC relay contacts. **Denial template** authorization matching narrow-path denial message from Ghost Fleet hail. Cross-timestamp matches Walsh notebook **S** column at eleven of fourteen logged windows. Three windows pending extraction. Zero contradictions so far."

Walsh, from secondary station: "Twelve of fourteen now. New fragment at 1012 matches boarding-day listen flatline logged 0714—distress arrived on narrow path because fleet band was administratively quiet, not physically dead."

Calder absorbed that. The distress Okonkwo sent had reached them on the only lane suppression policy had not managed to silence—because it was deprecated enough to be beneath the withhold template, or because someone had built honesty into a pipe the archive no longer believed in.

Either way, the crew had received it. That was not institutional mercy. That was Park and Cole making a thin lane count.


Okwelu cleared Okonkwo for second debrief at 1404—twenty minutes maximum, sitting only, medical monitor active. Calder attended with Park and Thessaly. Reyes joined for ten minutes at the start because buried-layer timestamps touched *Watcher's Mark* crew entries Okonkwo had authority to speak to. He left when Okwelu signaled time compression.

Okonkwo sat in medical bay with her hands folded on the blanket, steadier than yesterday, still not rested in any sense Okwelu would certify as recovery.

"Lieutenant Commander," Calder said. "Second debrief scope: suppression protocol as you operated under it. Not command-level motive. Not cosmology."

Okonkwo's mouth moved once—not quite a smile. "You learned fast."

"Buffer six class-three fragments name **suppress-and-reclassify** and **transmission withhold** orders. Interior Mandate routing. Your testimony: operational fact only."

Okonkwo looked at Park. "You peeled the buried layer."

Park: "Partial."

"Then you saw what we were told to do with numbers that disagreed." Okonkwo returned her gaze to Calder. "Interior Mandate issued mandate packets—not daily orders, standing policy refreshed on cycle. Field units logged operational truth locally. Institutional archive received template status and reclassified anomaly-adjacent reports into sealed partitions. Fleet Operations general visibility: revoked by design. Not accident. Not lag. Policy."

Calder: "Your crew knew."

"Chief Kellan knew archive and field were split—he ran vault watch. Lieutenant Reyes—forward compartment, different surname from your navigator—thought the split was temporary mapping secrecy. Ensign Matu wrote staggered log entries because her instruments showed staggered results and Interior Mandate told us stagger was a reporting defect, not a physical fact." Okonkwo paused. "I am telling you what we were trained to do with contradiction. I am not telling you who decided contradiction required training."

Thessaly: "Denial templates on deprecated auth paths."

Okonkwo: "Authorized. We were instructed to treat narrow-path and deprecated-band traffic as legacy noise unless manually verified by Interior Mandate liaison. Liaison contact sheared from *Watcher's Mark* roster six months before breach. After that, narrow path was the only comm that still answered honestly, and we were not permitted to report that fact upward in language Fleet Operations general visibility would accept."

Park: "Manual verification never arrived."

"No," Okonkwo said. "Manual verification never arrived."

Calder: "Matu. Kellan. Lieutenant Reyes."

Okonkwo's face closed a fraction—the same fraction as yesterday. "Matu might still be forward if your primary array was right. Kellan is midships vacuum if neither array is lying. Lieutenant Reyes is forward debris. I did not see him go. I saw times disagree." She held Calder's gaze. "That is crew fact. It is not suppression proof. You already have suppression proof in buffer six. Do not ask me to merge them into one story. I refuse to make their deaths administratively convenient."

The command deck silence from medical bay was different from command deck silence—smaller room, same discipline.

Calder: "Understood."

Okwelu: "Four minutes."

Okonkwo: "One more operational fact, Captain. Suppression policy included **protection language** in every mandate packet—**epistemic stability**, **containment of interpretive hazard**, **prevent cascade reporting**. I was told we were protecting Fleet Operations from data that would force decisions before mapping phase closure. I was not told what decisions. I was not told who would be harmed by knowing. I followed procedure because the alternative was to broadcast contradictions on bands that would reclassify my crew as defect reporting and shut our vault access." She looked at her hands. "Procedure did not save them. Procedure saved the store you have in buffer six. Decide what that means without asking me to forgive the institution that wrote the packets."

Calder did not ask forgiveness. He did not ask motive. He filed **protection language** beside **suppress-and-reclassify**—related, unproven, waiting.

Okwelu ended the debrief at nineteen minutes. Calder left medical bay with Park and Thessaly, passed Walsh in Corridor C carrying meal packets toward the command deck, notebook under his arm, pencil behind his ear because he still could not whistle and had stopped pretending the pencil was anything other than a tool.

"Twelve of fourteen matches confirmed," Walsh said quietly as Calder passed. "Two pending extraction. I'll have them when Reyes clears sector twenty-two."

"Good work," Calder said.

Walsh looked surprised he had said it aloud—the same surprise Cole had worn yesterday. Then he nodded and kept walking.


The argument found Calder at 1630 on the command deck after suppression interim structure landed in buffer seven segregated review—fourteen Walsh matches, eleven class-three fragments peeled, three platform bus corroborations, one narrow-path denial template match, zero merge keys issued.

Thessaly did not call it an argument. She called it a recommendation requiring captain acknowledgment.

"Operational conclusion," she said. "Fleet information suppression in Korrath interior is documented fact in our segregated record. Not suspicion. Not inference from absence alone. Policy language, routing headers, timestamp correlation, survivor testimony on protocol mechanics—not motive, not command-level authorship, not cosmological cause."

Calder: "And your recommendation."

"Treat suppression as weather," Thessaly said. "Same category as contradictory sensors and lost relay. We navigate without expecting institutional merge to clean the sky. We act on local observational authority and segregated evidence. We do not wait for Fleet Operations to confirm what their own buried headers already admit."

Calder studied the buffer seven summary on his tablet—not merged, not complete, enough to feel heavy.

"That is a doctrine shift."

"It is an acknowledgment shift," Thessaly said. "You already act without fleet confirmation. You already segregate sources. You already assigned observational authority at KR-13 transit. What you have not done is name suppression as operational adversary instead of operational absence."

Calder looked at Park on relay. Park did not offer an opinion unsolicited. Cole's eyes stayed on the congestion map.

Calder's institutional conditioning said: confirm before accuse. Accusation without fleet channel was career artifact at best, mutiny language at worst.

Calder's mission record said: fleet channel was flat, narrow path carried denial templates, buffer six carried suppress-and-reclassify orders, Okonkwo survived because she refused merge, Walsh's notebook matched suppression windows before the headers were readable.

"Thessaly," he said. "If we name suppression as adversary, we cut the last thread of institutional escalation."

"We cut a thread that relay loss already severed," Thessaly replied. "Naming the weather does not make it strike harder. It lets the crew stop standing in the rain pretending they are dry because the manual says so."

Walsh, at secondary station, did not look up. Calder noticed Walsh's pencil had moved on the notebook page anyway—timestamp, 1634, column **S**, entry tick. The crew had learned to log arguments as weather events.

Calder: "Halden. Defensive posture unchanged?"

Halden: "Unchanged. Passive sweep clean since shadow contact fell below threshold. No new ghost contacts above confidence floor."

Calder: "Koss. Buffer integrity?"

Koss: "Buffers five, six, seven segregated. Checksums stable. No ingestion flags."

Calder: "Reyes. Navigation hold stable?"

Reyes: "Hold stable. Primary and redundant agree on position for first time in six cycles—local fix only, no merge authority implied."

Small mercy. Calder took it.

"Authorize operational conclusion in ship record," he said. "Classification: **Fleet information suppression confirmed as policy-level operational fact** within Korrath interior mission context. Scope: documented suppress-and-reclassify routing, transmission withhold orders, denial template authorization on deprecated paths, epistemic stability review language as institutional framing. Explicit exclusion: command-level motive, full conspiracy scope, cosmological cause. Segregated review only. No outbound transmission. No master log merge pending my counter-signature after buffer seven completion."

Thessaly: "Aye, Captain."

Park logged the timestamp locally. Cole whispered "logged" a half-second later, the way she whispered congestion before the display confirmed.

Calder felt the decision land—not certainty, enough confidence. Scout doctrine rarely offered both.


Final extraction on buffer six class-three layers completed at 1912 with Tanaka catching a sector boundary stagger error at twenty-one before it propagated—same discipline as boarding ingestion, same human precision beneath the institutional ugliness.

Walsh's last two **S** column matches arrived at 1927.

"Fourteen of fourteen," he reported from secondary station. "Final fragment header **FLEET OPS / INTERIOR MANDATE / SUPPRESS-AND-RECLASSIFY** with subrouting **KISC-INT-SEAL-7**. Timestamp matches Okwelu pass-through logged 1411 in Corridor C—not because Okwelu caused suppression. Because suppression windows correlate with crew fatigue events Walsh logged before Thessaly adjusted watches. Secondary pattern. Not causal proof. Operational texture."

Thessaly: "Document the secondary pattern in buffer seven appendix. Walsh attribution."

Walsh nodded. He did not smile. He closed the notebook to the ribbon marker and slid it into the pocket of his coveralls—the way Brenner slid a grounded case away when the pins were seated.

Reyes presented the consolidated suppression index at 1948—not a fleet document, a *Veil* document, segregated, checksum-logged, built from buffer five platform reconstruction, buffer six buried transmissions, buffer seven notebook correlation, Okonkwo debrief fragments on protocol mechanics, and Park's narrow-path denial template capture from Ghost Fleet hail.

"Human-readable conclusion," Reyes said after the nod. "Fleet Operations, through Interior Mandate division routing, operated **information suppression** as standing policy in Korrath interior survey context. Anomaly-adjacent reporting was reclassified, not deleted. Relay and archive visibility were revoked by design. Denial templates were authorized on deprecated authentication paths. Field units were instructed to treat contradiction as reporting defect until Interior Mandate manual verification—verification that ceased to arrive for *Watcher's Mark* six months before hull breach. Suppression is **operational fact**. Full motive and command authorship remain **unresolved**."

The command deck was quiet the way the deck went quiet when a problem had been solved by people rather than by institutional contact.

Calder reviewed the index once—not for merge, for weight.

"Print methodology to ship record," he said. "Buffer seven sealed. Walsh notebook returns to Walsh. Photographs remain backup. Okwelu holds Okonkwo off third debrief until medical clearance. Park—narrow path listen cycle unchanged. Halden—defensive watch unchanged. Meal cycle on schedule."

Walsh: "Meal cycle already on schedule, Captain."

Thessaly's mouth moved once—not quite a smile either. "He means me."

Walsh delivered meal packets without disrupting workflow. Calder ate half a ration standing and reviewed the suppression index summary on his tablet—not merged, not fleet-facing, true enough to act inside.


At 2140, Park reported narrow-path carrier rise—session stub, payload burst, termination in spec-compliant order. Cole identified the burst by sound before the display confirmed. Park captured into buffer five subpartition G—still segregated, still no merge—new plaintext fragment, administrative, partial.

Reyes read it aloud after the nod.

"—**epistemic stability preservation remains paramount. Suppression is protective measure, not punitive. Recall authority reserved Interior Mandate command tier sheared.** End fragment."

Protection language again. Not an answer. A frame.

Calder stood on the command deck with the suppression index sealed in buffer seven, Walsh's fourteen-of-fourteen correlation logged, Okonkwo's professional testimony filed without forced synthesis, and a new fragment that said **protective measure** in the same administrative voice that had authorized denial templates and suppress-and-reclassify routing.

Thessaly joined him at the rail—not close enough to be comfort, close enough to be witness.

"You got your operational fact," she said quietly.

"Yes."

"And the question Fleet did not put in the index."

Calder watched the narrow-path indicator return to flat line. Relay to L-3 remained lost. Ghost Fleet contacts remained inconclusive. *Watcher's Mark* drifted damaged somewhere aft with three restraint clips and one empty helmet and crew who had vanished on incompatible clocks. Okonkwo slept in medical bay under Okwelu's watch. Walsh's notebook was in Walsh's pocket. Buffer six held the store she had sealed. Buffer seven held the proof that absence had been policy.

"Protection or cause," Calder said.

Thessaly did not answer. Scout doctrine did not require her to. The question was not for the XO to resolve. It was for the captain to carry until the crew earned the next fragment—or until the weather made the question moot.

Calder logged the fragment in segregated review and closed the tablet.

The silent war had given them one victory: they could name what the institution had done with information when reality refused to cooperate.

It had not told them whether suppression had been shielding humanity from something too large to merge—or sharpening the edge of whatever had cut *Watcher's Mark* apart.

That question would follow him into Running Dark.

---


The chapter's revelation was institutional, not cosmological.

Cross-work on buffer six buried class-three transmission layers, buffer five platform reconstruction, Park's narrow-path captures, and Walsh's independent watch-notebook timestamp correlation established **Fleet information suppression as operational fact** within Korrath interior survey context. Recovered policy language documented **suppress-and-reclassify** routing for anomaly-adjacent reporting, **transmission withhold** orders for KISC relay contacts, **denial template** authorization on deprecated authentication paths, and **epistemic stability review** framing—explicit institutional vocabulary for withholding merge-visible truth while preserving reclassified archive copies.

Walsh's personal watch notebook—used by Thessaly as unofficial schedule intelligence—provided independent temporal corroboration at fourteen of fourteen logged suppression-adjacent windows, including congestion and flat-band events preceding readable header extraction. Buffer seven segregated review captured methodology without master log merge.

Lieutenant Commander Okonkwo's second debrief supplied professional testimony on suppression **protocol mechanics**—Interior Mandate mandate packets, field/archive split, manual verification failure—not command-level motive, authorship, or resolution of *Watcher's Mark* crew fate. She explicitly refused to merge survivor tragedy into administrative convenience.

Calder and Thessaly's doctrine tension resolved in an acknowledgment shift: treat suppression as navigable weather alongside contradictory sensors and lost relay, act on observational authority and segregated evidence, name policy-level suppression without claiming full conspiracy scope.

A late narrow-path fragment added **protective measure** language without resolving intent. Suppression was confirmed as something Fleet did. Whether it protected humanity or contributed to catastrophe remained open—the chapter's carrying question.

---


The *Kestrel Veil* held at KR-14 vicinity on passive drift. Relay contact to L-3 remained lost. Narrow-path handshake verified; listen-capture into buffer five subpartition G continued under segregated protocol. Hull intact. Drive operational. Sensor reliability low—primary/redundant position agreement on local hold only, not restored merge authority.

Buffer five (platform survey), buffer six (KISU-7 core store), and buffer seven (suppression index and Walsh notebook photographic backup) remained segregated, barred from master log merge pending Calder counter-signature after buffer seven completion. Four KR-12 buffers unchanged.

Lieutenant Commander Okonkwo remained aboard under Okwelu's medical authority—second debrief complete, third debrief held pending clearance.

Calder Venn authorized operational conclusion naming Fleet information suppression as confirmed policy-level fact within documented scope, explicitly excluding command motive and full conspiracy resolution. Thessaly's acknowledgment-shift doctrine—treat suppression as weather, act on observational authority—entered active command posture.

Human victory: crew exposed suppression as operational fact through multi-source correlation—Reyes/Ortega buried-layer extraction, Park/Cole crypto peel, Walsh/Thessaly timestamp intelligence, Okonkwo protocol testimony, Koss hardware corroboration—without outbound transmission or institutional merge collapse.

Ghost Fleet contacts unchanged inconclusive. *Watcher's Mark* drifting damaged, not recovered. Cohesion: strained with second task-success anchor after boarding extraction.

Calder carried the suppression index forward and the sharper question with it:

Had Fleet been suppressing information to **protect** humanity from interpretive cascade—or had suppression itself become part of what killed compartmentalized crews?

Protection or cause.

He would carry that question forward.

---

\newpage

────────────────────────────────────────

# ~ CHAPTER TEN ~

## Running Dark

────────────────────────────────────────



Thirty-one hours had passed since Walsh matched the third encrypted stub to a suppression header Park had already logged in buffer six cross-reference.

The match was not a conspiracy map. It was worse in the way operational facts were worse than theories: a dated Fleet Operations relay tag—**ANOMALY REPORT / HOLD FOR INTERIOR REVIEW**—sitting on top of a Korrath interior survey packet that should have propagated to L-3 in 2224 and had instead vanished from every merge path Okonkwo's core store said existed. Walsh had found it because his watch notebook tracked handshake windows Thessaly used for scheduling and Park used for narrow-path verification, and the windows did not align with the timestamps on the encrypted captures. Cole had heard the congestion profile first. Park had verified checksum. Thessaly had ordered the comparison logged without outbound transmission.

Suppression was no longer suspicion. It was something the crew could act inside—the same way they acted inside contradictory star fixes.

Calder had not slept since Walsh closed the loop at 0419 on the previous cycle. Okwelu would note it. He would acknowledge and change nothing. Some patterns survived because they worked until they did not.

The *Kestrel Veil* had departed KR-14 vicinity at 0912 on minimal thruster burn after Okwelu cleared Okonkwo for ship transfer to secured medical quarters and Koss certified buffer six isolation through departure load. SCV-219 *Watcher's Mark* remained on passive track astern—a damaged silhouette the redundant array sometimes placed closer than primary and sometimes forgot entirely. Calder had stopped asking which array owned the ghost. He asked what action remained valid regardless.

Shadow contacts had returned.

Not the inconclusive blip Halden logged during boarding extraction. A pattern. Three passive tracks on primary bearing solutions that held relative geometry too cleanly for drift error. One additional contact on redundant sweep that primary did not always see. No emission spikes. No hails on fleet bands. No narrow-path traffic except the verified stub Park maintained on forty-minute cycle—thin, honest, and useless for asking Fleet whether three unknown hulls had permission to follow a scout that official records still believed held at Korrath fringe.

Reyes called them **followers** in the shadow log and refused to merge the term into master navigation.

Halden called them **superior** once, in a department head brief Calder still tasted: mass profiles consistent with corvette or light escort class, closure rates ambiguous between arrays, weapons signatures absent because passive posture did not grant weapons truth and active sweep would grant position.

Calder had authorized continued outbound drift on thrusters-only separation stack—no jump prep, no high-emission maneuvering, no fleet ping. Thessaly held command timeline. Park kept the narrow path alive without using it to shout. Koss held engineering ring draw below three percent and watched Sharma's life-support readouts the way she watched bearing fatigue in the drive harmonics.

Sharma had been on berth environmental control since 1147—twenty-two hours by Calder's count, though Koss would have corrected him to twenty-two hours and eleven minutes because Koss corrected time the way Brenner corrected torque sequences. Sharma's certification tablet showed atmosphere scrubber duty cycles shifted into emissions-adjacent load management: CO₂ reclamation staged down, thermal vent routing through passive baffles, galley alcove air handlers on minimum exchange because even filtered exhaust left a thermal signature Koss would not certify clean at scout-class resolution.

Calder had learned Sharma's name during boarding extraction. He was learning her competence now the way he learned Park's—by watching what broke when she was absent.

In the galley alcove an hour before departure, Park had refilled Halden's coffee. Halden had not joked. Park had not commented. Calder had eaten standing and reviewed Walsh's notebook photocopy on his tablet—not the original; Walsh had not surrendered the original since Thessaly borrowed it once without telling him and he had noticed anyway.

"Brenner's birthday," Park had said quietly, passing Calder on the way to relay.

Calder had looked up.

"Fourth of cycle. You still don't know."

"I know now."

Park had almost smiled. Almost. Then the shadow contact update had come from Halden and the almost had gone with it.

Okonkwo remained under Okwelu's authority in medical bay—limited consciousness hours, full debrief deferred. Her name sat in buffer six personnel strings beside Kellan and Matu and Lieutenant Reyes who was not their navigator. Calder carried that fragmentation without merging it.

Buffer five held platform reconstruction. Buffer six held KISU-7. Four KR-12 buffers unchanged. Master log reconciliation eighteen percent uncorroborated. Relay mostly lost. Narrow path verified. Suppression confirmed as operational fact.

And three—possibly four—contacts holding geometry on a scout-class hull that had just proved Fleet headers lied.

Calder stood at the command console in second watch with corridor lighting at full and every display alive and understood, with the slow cold clarity that had replaced institutional certainty, that alive displays were an invitation.

He had not yet ordered darkness.

He was almost ready to decide he did not need more certainty to do it.

---


**Primary Mission Objective:**

Execute passive egress from KR-14 departure corridor without engagement. Maintain segregated buffer integrity and narrow-path comm discipline under emissions control. Deny shadowing forces positive identification and weapons solution while transiting to KR-13 segment fallback coordinates using local observation navigation only. Preserve crew function and life-support viability under reduced power and thermal signature constraints.

---



Halden's brief at 0633 was not dramatic. Calder trusted that about him.

"Primary array holds three contacts, bearing cluster aft-port relative, closure rate zero on last four sweeps if primary range is authoritative. Redundant array holds four contacts, same bearing cluster, closure rate low positive on two of four over the same interval." Halden's voice did not rise. "Mass profiles escort-class or better. No emission spikes. No fleet authentication on any band Park monitors. Shadow log preserves both solutions. I am not merging."

Reyes, without looking up from the local chart overlay: "If they are not closing on primary, they are pacing. If redundant closure is real, they are herding."

"Herding toward what?" Thessaly asked.

Reyes: "Unknown. I can give you local observation fixes on KR-13 segment two landmarks—platform debris scatter from KISU-7 charts, thermal null zones Okonkwo's store marks as **stable dark**. Not fleet charts. Local."

Ortega: "Primary places the nearest landmark fix at six hours sublight on current drift. Redundant places it at three."

Calder heard the familiar disagreement and did not treat it as novelty. He treated it as weather.

"Park. Narrow path status."

"Verified on last handshake. No inbound fleet traffic on path. Fleet bands silent except intermittent fragment on low sideband—" Park paused, head tilted a degree Cole would have recognized. "—checksum unstable. I am not certifying content. I hear presence. I do not hear words yet."

"Copy." Calder looked at Halden. "Defensive posture."

"Passive held. Hale on emitter standby—not hot. I will not joke about it."

He had not joked. Calder noted the absence the way Park did.

"Koss."

Engineering channel, flat and immediate: "Ring draw at two-point-eight percent. Sharma has scrubber staging ready for your order. Brenner is on vent baffle alignment in ring four. We can go dark on your mark. Going dark is not free. Berth CO₂ rises. Thermal sinks slower. Okwelu should hear the life-support trade before you authorize."

"Summarize trade for command."

Sharma's voice—younger than Brenner's, careful: "At full emissions control, atmosphere margin drops from nine-point-two days to six-point-eight at current complement if galley and medical bay hold reduced exchange. Thermal vent through baffles adds four hours to sink equilibrium per Koss's model. I can keep medical bay on priority scrubber if Okwelu confirms Okonkwo's compartment isolation."

Okwelu, from medical channel without being summoned: "Confirmed. Medical holds priority. I want Sharma's numbers every ninety minutes logged to my tablet, not summarized."

Sharma: "Yes, Doctor. I— copy."

Calder did not correct the apology reflex. He had larger failures to prevent.

"Thessaly. Recommendation."

She had already built it. "We cannot outrun escort-class on thrusters if they commit. We can deny them a clean lock if we stop advertising. Shadow contacts have held geometry eighteen hours without hail— that is not Fleet courtesy. That is surveillance. Egress on local observation to KR-13 dark landmarks buys time and distance from KR-14 volume where Interior Mandate fiction still has infrastructure. Walsh logs watch windows. Park keeps narrow path cold—listen, do not speak. Calder decides emissions mark."

Calder looked at the forward display—stars, drift vector, three contacts rendered as probability cones rather than icons because icons pretended certainty.

He did not have unified sensor truth. He had a crew that had extracted a survivor, proven suppression, and learned to act on enough confidence.

"Prepare emissions control package," he said. "Reyes plots local observation egress. Halden preserves shadow log on both arrays. Park—passive listen only on narrow path after mark. No outbound. Koss and Sharma execute on my mark. Command deck lighting to dark-watch preset at mark plus thirty seconds. Thessaly holds timeline."

"Aye, Captain," Thessaly said.

Tanaka had already opened her color-coded checklist to the page Reyes had not yet turned to confirm—the current page because the ship was always one confirmed segment behind where it needed to be.

---


Calder waited eleven minutes after the prepare order—not for certainty, for Brenner's confirmation on vent baffle torque and Sharma's final scrubber staging read.

Koss: "Ring four baffles seated. Harmonic clean. Sharma?"

Sharma: "Atmosphere staging at dark-run profile. Medical priority online. Galley exchange at minimum. Berth temp will feel wrong before it becomes wrong—I will tell you when it becomes wrong."

Calder appreciated that distinction. Wrong-feeling was weather aboard a scout. Wrong-measured was his problem.

Reyes: "Local observation plot locked to KISU-7 segment two scatter and thermal null **stable dark** waypoint. No fleet chart merge. Tanaka has sequence on my mark."

Halden: "Contacts holding. Primary closure still zero. Redundant low positive on two tracks. If they are herding, darkness is the correct counter."

Calder did not ask Halden to repeat it. He asked himself whether he trusted interpretive models from people who had been right when merge authority was not.

He did.

"Mark," he said.

Koss: "Emissions control engaged."

The command deck changed before the displays did—a subsonic shift in airflow as scrubbers stepped down, a faint drop in lamp output as lighting cycled to dark-watch preset thirty seconds later, the almost-silence of a ship that had stopped explaining itself to the vacuum.

Sharma on channel, voice lower as if volume could leak signature: "Scrubber profile confirmed. Thermal vent routing through baffles. First sink equilibrium estimate in four hours twelve minutes if nobody opens a hot compartment without clearance."

"Authorized personnel only," Thessaly said. "Walsh, post on environmental doors. Brenner exception for ring four."

Walsh: "Posted."

Calder watched passive sensor overlays populate—no active sweep, no ping, only what the hull could hear when it stopped shouting. Three contacts on primary. Four on redundant. Probability cones widening, not narrowing. Honest uncertainty.

Park at relay, headset adjusted: "Fleet bands flat. Narrow path listening. Sideband fragment returned once—dropped before checksum. I hear traffic the way you hear weather before rain. Not words."

"Log presence. Do not chase."

"Copy."

Reyes leaned into the local chart—not the master navigation merge, the shadow overlay he and Tanaka maintained in margin notation Reyes had carried since *Meridian Echo*. His finger traced a line that did not exist on fleet charts.

"First segment on thrusters only," he said. "Ninety minutes at current drift adjustment. Ortega confirms thermal null bearing on primary when we have enough passive accumulation."

Ortega: "Accumulation window eight minutes minimum at this posture. I—" Reyes's look. "—eight minutes minimum, Captain."

Calder: "Execute."

The *Veil* did not burn. It adjusted—a whisper of thruster exhale Calder felt through the console rail more than heard. Scout-class ships were built to be subtle when survival required it. The crew had simply never required it at this scale.

Halden began building threat models on the tactical side display—not weapons solutions, because they had no clean targets and no authorization to create them. Closure envelopes. Bearing drift. Scenarios where redundant range was truth versus scenarios where primary pacing was truth. He worked without commentary. Without jokes.

Calder watched Halden's silence and understood the humor had not stopped because the situation was dire. It had stopped because Halden had decided jokes were a form of emission.

---


Hour two of dark running was Sharma's hour in the way boarding extraction had been Cole's— not because Calder gave her the deck, but because every department head eventually routed through her numbers.

CO₂ crept from zero-point-four to zero-point-seven percent berth aggregate. Thermal sinks lagged four hours behind Koss's model by twenty-one minutes. Sharma reported both without dramatizing.

"Galley alcove feels stale," she told Koss on the engineering channel. "Not out of spec. Stale."

Koss: "Keep exchange at minimum. Tell Calder if spec approaches."

Sharma: "I will tell Captain Venn at zero-point-nine aggregate or if medical bay pressure delta exceeds my tablet threshold."

Calder heard the exchange because engineering routed through his console by default and because Sharma had learned to say his name without apologizing for saying it—a small victory Okwelu might have classified as morale and Koss would classify as correct procedure.

In the passageway outside ring four, Brenner passed Sharma a wrench she had not asked for—the correct wrench, pre-staged the way Brenner staged everything he called optimized retrieval.

"Baffle harmonic shifted two minutes ago," he said. "I fixed it. You did not owe me a call because your scrubber staging caught the load before the harmonic became a fault. Good work."

Sharma's pause lasted one second. "Thank you. I had the staging read early. Koss wanted margin."

"Koss always wants margin. Margin is why we're breathing."

He left. Sharma logged the exchange in her certification tablet under **dark run / emissions trade** and did not apologize to the tablet.

Calder read the log entry because Sharma's tablet synced to engineering summary by protocol. He filed Brenner's sentence under crew gravity—the way Park knew birthdays and Cole knew congestion by sound and Walsh knew who would refuse to leave a station.

Okwelu appeared in the command deck doorway at hour two plus forty—not entering, not summoning, documenting.

"Berth aggregate acceptable," she said. "Medical bay stable. Okonkwo sleeping. Sharma's ninety-minute logs are accurate. I am adding one hour to Cole's next rest window because Park has not rotated off listen and her blink rate is wrong."

Park, without turning: "Blink rate is fine."

Okwelu: "No."

Park rotated off listen long enough to meet Okwelu's gaze. "Copy."

Calder: "Status."

Okwelu: "You are running dark successfully. That is not the same as safe. Neither is medical."

She left. Calder returned to the passive overlay.

Reyes reported segment one complete on local observation—waypoint not reached, but drift correction consistent with KISU-7 scatter chart, not fleet merge. Tanaka logged the sequence without celebration. Ortega's thermal null bearing on primary matched Reyes's margin fix within tolerance Calder would once have called insufficient. He now called it enough to act.

Halden: "Contact geometry update. Primary still pacing—closure zero. Redundant closure on two tracks increased low positive to low-medium over last sweep window. If redundant is herding, they expect us to run where they can measure run. Darkness deprives them of half their argument."

"And the other half?" Calder asked.

Halden did not joke. "Mass and motion. We still have both. We are not advertising. That is Sharma's work and Koss's baffles. That is Hale keeping emitters cold. That is you not pinging Fleet for permission."

Calder felt the rebuke without resentment. It was accurate.

Park, barely audible: "Sideband fragment again. Three syllables maybe. Checksum failed. I cannot certify words. I hear—" Another pause. "—intermittent traffic. Like someone testing the edge of the lane without committing. Not fleet hail. Not narrow-path handshake."

"Log it."

"Logged."

Cole, at secondary relay in the dim, mug absent for once: "Congestion profile on the fragment is thin. Real thin. If they're shadowing us, they may be listening to the same weather."

Calder: "Assume they are. Assume everyone listens."

Thessaly moved along the deck perimeter—the XO walk, presence without noise. Walsh swapped with Hale at defensive secondary without conversation beyond the notebook handoff. Brenner checked ring four once and returned to passageway station. The ship contracted to whispers and function.

Calder realized he had not checked fleet contact in ninety minutes because there was no fleet contact to check—only Park's intermittent traffic like rain on a roof you could not yet see.

---


Hour four brought the first moment Calder understood they were being driven.

Reyes saw it in local observation first—not in the contact cones, in the drift corridor. Thermal null zones that should have offered cover skewed relative to passive thermal background when Ortega stacked eight-minute accumulation windows.

"They are not closing on primary," Reyes said quietly. "They are shaping our drift. Redundant closure tracks correlate with null zones shifting aft. Something active at extreme low power—or something using mass shadow we cannot resolve—pushing thermal background."

Halden: "Threat model update. Scenario B—herding confirmed on redundant interpretation. Primary pacing remains plausible decoy. Recommend segment two adjustment seven degrees port relative to KISU-7 chart. Uses debris scatter occluder Okonkwo's store marks at segment two boundary."

Calder looked at the plot. Seven degrees port on thrusters-only was not evasion in the heroic sense. It was a clerk's move—small, documentable in local observation, invisible to anyone who trusted fleet charts that said this volume was empty.

"Authorize," he said.

Thruster whisper again. Sharma reported vent baffle load spike within tolerance. Koss: "Handled."

Primary contacts held pacing. Redundant contacts on two tracks—brief closure spike, then relaxation.

Calder's pulse did not spike. His decision cycle did—shorter, almost blind, built from Halden's scenario B and Reyes's occluder and Sharma's margin numbers and Park's intermittent traffic rather than from any institutional merge.

"Did we show?" he asked.

Halden: "No weapons lock. No active sweep from contacts. Passive only on our side. On theirs—unknown. We did not illuminate."

Thessaly: "That is the victory condition until it is not."

Calder almost asked her to define victory. He did not. He knew.

Human victory was not destruction of followers. It was denial—no engagement, no capture, no clean identification transmitted to whatever Fleet layer had suppressed anomaly reports and compartmentalized Okonkwo's crew into incompatible clocks.

Segment two adjustment completed at 1447. Ortega confirmed occluder thermal profile on primary within Reyes's tolerance. Redundant array placed the occluder closer and thicker—a familiar argument.

Park: "Traffic again. Longer burst. Still no checksum. I think—" She stopped.

Calder waited.

"I think I heard a callsign fragment. Obsolete prefix. Not our narrow path. Not fleet band. I am not certifying."

Halden's jaw tightened once. "Ghost Fleet weather."

"Log as uncertified presence," Calder said. "Do not merge into threat model as confirmed hostile. Log as pressure."

Halden: "Logged."

Okwelu sent one line to Calder's tablet from medical: **Okonkwo awake. Brief window if needed. Do not wake ship for it.**

He did not wake the ship for it.

---


Hour six was when redundant closure spiked and primary did not—and the crew chose darkness over battle without discussing it as philosophy.

Halden: "Redundant reports two contacts accelerating closure—medium range on redundant, long on primary. Divergence at maximum since KR-12. If redundant is right, we have eleven minutes before their passive solution tightens to escort intervention range. If primary is right, we have an hour."

Reyes: "Segment three option—thermal null **stable dark** waypoint at KISU-7 mark. Eleven minutes on thrusters at whisper if we accept CO₂ margin burn from Sharma."

Sharma, already speaking before Koss could: "CO₂ margin burn for eleven minutes drops aggregate from six-point-eight days to six-point-one if we restore exchange after segment. Medical bay holds priority. I can do it. I need Brenner on ring four baffles for thirty seconds at peak vent."

Koss: "Show me the tablet."

A pause—Sharma pushing her certification screen to Koss's engineering console. Calder did not see the numbers. He heard Koss's silence, the good kind.

"Margin is tight but real," Koss said. "Not fiction. Sharma, you are authorized to spend it."

Sharma: "Copy. Brenner on baffles at peak."

Koss: "Brenner."

Brenner: "Ready."

Calder had eleven minutes of contradictory range and six-point-one days of air and no fleet authority and a suppression header Walsh had matched to a lie.

He did not have certainty. He had enough confidence.

"Execute segment three," he said. "Halden—track both solutions. No active sweep. No weapons hot. Hale stays cold."

Hale: "Cold."

Thruster whisper became thruster breath—still not a burn, not engagement, the scout equivalent of holding your mouth open and moving anyway. Sharma's tablet spiked yellow, then green. Brenner reported baffle peak handled. Koss reported harmonic clean.

On passive overlay, redundant closure tightened—then broke geometry.

Primary contacts held pacing.

Redundant contacts—two of four—dropped below threshold on redundant sweep entirely while primary still placed them aft-port at long range.

Calder watched probability cones diverge and did not merge them.

"They lost redundant solution," Halden said. Voice flat. "Primary pacing unchanged. We are still in the window."

"Or they stopped herding on redundant because we stopped feeding the array they expected," Reyes said.

"Or weather," Park said.

Calder: "We do not need motive. We need distance."

Segment three completed. Local observation placed the *Veil* at KISU-7 **stable dark** waypoint margin—not inside the null, adjacent, the way Reyes navigated by occluder and scatter rather than by fleet certainty.

Halden ran threat models again. Closure envelopes widened on primary. Redundant remained inconsistent—contacts absent, then one returned, then two.

"No weapons lock," Halden said. "No hail. No emission spike from contacts."

Thessaly, almost whisper: "Human victory is ugly today."

Calder heard the boarding extraction in her sentence. He answered the dark run they were still inside.

"Today it counts."

---


Hour eight. Emissions control engaged. Lights dimmed on dark-watch preset until the command deck existed in the blue-gray of instruments and faces reduced to cheekbone and habit. Calder's console cast enough glow to read threat envelopes without painting the bulkhead. Somewhere in ring four, a vent baffle ticked once—metal cooling, Brenner's work settling into the new equilibrium Sharma had bought them.

No Fleet contact—flat bands, silent relay, narrow path listening cold. Calder had stopped expecting acknowledgment. The absence was its own kind of answer: whatever Fleet had been protecting with **HOLD FOR INTERIOR REVIEW** did not include telling a scout in Korrath whether darkness was permitted.

Passive sensors only—primary and redundant preserved in shadow log, never merged, never used to shout. The ship listened the way Park listened—patient, uncertified, unwilling to pretend silence meant empty.

Unknown contacts on scope—three on primary, four on redundant, probability cones like bruises aft-port. They had not vanished. They had not committed. They remained geometry, and geometry was what superior forces used when they believed time belonged to them.

Crew whispering on deck—Tanaka confirming segment markers to Reyes in words Calder had to lean to hear; Hale reporting emitter temperature to Halden in monosyllables; Cole telling Park the sideband was quiet for now, for now being the only promise the relay could keep.

Reyes at navigation, local chart overlay glowing at minimum luminance, navigating by observation fixes Okonkwo's store had smuggled out of Interior Mandate compartmentalization—thermal nulls, debris scatter, occluder marks Tanaka sequenced in color only she and Reyes fully shared.

Halden at tactical secondary building threat models without jokes—closure scenarios, herding vectors, weapons-lock thresholds they had not crossed because Hale kept emitters cold and Calder kept authority off fleet ping.

Park at relay with Cole shadowing one station back, barely hearing intermittent traffic on sideband fragments—presence without words, checksum failures logged as weather.

Calder at the command console making decisions almost blind—trusting Sharma's CO₂ margin at six-point-one days, trusting Koss's baffles, trusting Reyes's local waypoint over fleet merge, trusting Halden's silence as seriously as he had once trusted institutional headers.

Sharma reported from ring environmental: "Aggregate stable post-burn. Exchange restoring to dark-run minimum. Medical bay priority holding. Berth still feels stale. Not wrong yet."

Calder: "Copy. Log every ninety to Okwelu."

"Yes, Captain."

She did not apologize.

Walsh passed along the deck perimeter with his notebook closed for once—watch assignments suspended in dark run except where Thessaly posted them. Okwelu sat in the auxiliary chair angled seventeen degrees from vertical for six minutes before returning to medical without comment. Brenner's heel strike did not appear on environmental door 7-C; Calder had learned not to test that door alone.

The ship held breath.

Contacts paced on primary. Redundant flickered—one contact, two, none, two again. No weapons lock. No battle. No engagement—only shadowing and darkness and professional fear without panic.

Calder looked at the forward display—not stars as certainty, stars as context. The *Kestrel Veil* was uncaught. Not safe. Uncaught in a volume where suppression was fact and followers were geometry and Fleet would not answer because Fleet had been holding anomaly reports for Interior Review since before Okonkwo sealed her vault.

He did not order lights up. He did not order active sweep. He did not ask Park to speak on the narrow path.

Emissions control engaged. Passive only. Unknown contacts on scope. Crew whispering. Reyes navigating by local observation. Halden building threat models. Park barely hearing intermittent traffic. Calder deciding almost blind.

Held breath.

Calder did not look back at KR-14. The damaged *Watcher's Mark* and the platform vault and the compartmentalized lies were behind them in distance if not in consequence. Ahead was darkness the charts disagreed about and followers who had not introduced themselves. He thought of Lisette on Helion Prime receiving his scheduled relay messages into a void he could not yet explain to her without merging truths he had not earned.

He thought of Sharma's grandmother on Helion Prime getting weekly messages from a granddaughter who was currently spending CO₂ margin so the ship would not shine.

He did not think long. Long thinking was a luxury emissions control had revoked.

The unanswered question was what the followers wanted them to run toward in the dark—and whether **stable dark** was a waypoint or a door.

Passive sensor tone shifted once—soft, the kind Park said meant a band had stopped pretending to be empty.

Halden's voice, barely above a whisper: "They're done watching."

Calder did not look back at KR-14. He looked at the scope.

Held breath broke.

---


The chapter's operational revelation was not cosmological. It was tactical and human at once.

Shadowing forces—likely escort-class or superior by mass profile, unauthenticated, non-hailing—had maintained surveillance geometry on the *Kestrel Veil* for more than thirty hours following KR-14 departure. Under Calder's emissions control order, the crew executed a full dark-running posture: passive sensors only, no fleet transmission, narrow-path listen-only discipline, life-support and thermal signature reduction managed by Sharma under Koss's engineering authority.

The crew **evaded engagement** through combined competence—Reyes local observation navigation using KISU-7 chart marks outside fleet merge; Halden dual-array threat modeling without weapons escalation; Sharma's emissions-life-support tradeoffs preserving six-point-one days atmosphere margin through segment burn; Brenner baffle maintenance; Hale cold emitters; Park uncertified traffic logging; Walsh/Thessaly watch discipline carried from suppression verification.

Redundant array closure spikes suggested **herding behavior**—contacts shaping drift toward predictable corridors—while primary array pacing remained consistent with surveillance rather than immediate intercept. Segment three whisper maneuver broke redundant closure geometry without triggering weapons lock on either array interpretation.

This was not resolution of Signal Deviation. It was confirmation that human crews could deny superior shadowing forces a clean engagement window inside anomaly weather—if they stopped advertising and trusted interpretive models over institutional merge.

Whether followers were Fleet, Ghost Fleet, or third-party remained open. Whether **stable dark** waypoints offered safety or entrapment remained open.

---


The *Kestrel Veil* held at KISU-7 segment two **stable dark** waypoint margin under full emissions control—dark-watch lighting, passive sensors, narrow path listen-only. Relay contact absent. No Fleet acknowledgment. Shadow contacts persist on primary; redundant solutions inconsistent.

Buffers five and six segregated; KR-12 set unchanged. Okonkwo under medical authority. Suppression confirmed operational fact from Walsh notebook / encrypted stub verification chain.

Calder Venn authorized dark running on interpretive crew models without fleet confirmation or unified sensor truth—decisions almost blind, confidence sufficient to act. Human victory: crew evaded superior shadowing forces without engagement—uncaught, not safe.

Cohesion: strained with task-success anchor strengthened by evasion. Stress high across command deck; Park on intermittent traffic edge; Halden humor absent by choice.

Mission clarity low; operational competence rising. Next inevitability: weapons lock.

Protection or cause—the suppression question from Interior Mandate evidence—remains unanswered at command level.

What are the followers herding them toward?

Held breath broke on Halden's warning. Weapons lock was next.

---

\newpage

────────────────────────────────────────

# ~ CHAPTER ELEVEN ~

## The First Engagement

────────────────────────────────────────



Held breath broke on Halden's warning. Weapons lock was next.

The forward display had not changed color yet. Calder's hand was already on the command rail—the unconscious posture of a man who had spent thirty-one hours deciding in darkness and had known, for the last forty seconds, that darkness was a loan.

Ortega spoke first, because passive sweep was her truth and she did not wait for permission to report it.

"Active emission spike, contact two—primary array only. Redundant still shows cold. Band consistent with target illumination, not hail."

Hale's panel chimed once—the sound Calder had not heard since boarding extraction prep at KR-14, when the world still pretended unknown hulls might be curious rather than committed.

"Weapons lock warning, aft-port cone," Hale said. No drama in it. Checklist voice. "Margaret's unhappy. I'm still cold unless you say otherwise."

Margaret was the name Hale had given his favorite emitter array. Nobody else used it. Calder heard it now the way he heard Reyes say **followers**—operational vocabulary the crew had earned because fleet merge had stopped earning anything.

Halden did not look at Calder. He looked at threat envelopes.

"Lock quality rising on primary. Redundant contact count just dropped to one, then zero, then three. Geometry tightening. They're not pacing anymore."

Thessaly was already moving—XO walk accelerating into reorganized stations before Calder finished forming the word he needed.

"Battle stations," Calder said.

Not a speech. A category change.

Lights shifted from dark-watch blue-gray to combat minimum—enough to read blood if blood became relevant, not enough to paint the hull for anyone who had already decided to see them. Somewhere in ring four, Sharma's atmosphere processors surged under new load orders Koss had pre-staged for exactly this failure mode. Brenner's heel strike appeared on Calder's peripheral threat board as movement toward junction seven—not panic, route.

Park at relay: "Sideband traffic just went loud. Not fleet. Not narrow path. Checksum garbage on first pass."

"Log it," Calder said.

"Already am. Some of it's noise. Some of it isn't."

That was Park's job today—separate signal from noise in a volume where noise might be weapon.

Calder had not slept since Walsh closed the suppression loop. Okwelu would note the new timestamp. He would acknowledge and change nothing until the ship either survived or did not. Lisette on Helion Prime remained a scheduled message in a buffer he might never transmit cleanly again.

The *Kestrel Veil* held at KISU-7 segment two **stable dark** waypoint margin with three primary shadow contacts and a redundant array that could not agree on how many gods were watching. Emissions control had been engaged for eight hours. Passive sensors only. Crew whispering. Reyes navigating by local observation marks Okonkwo's stolen store had smuggled out of Interior Mandate compartmentalization.

All of that ended when contact two lit them up.

Not safe. Never safe. Uncaught had been the victory of the last watch.

This was what happened when the followers stopped lending time.

---


**Primary Mission Objective:**

Break weapons lock geometry, deny hostile force a clean intercept, and egress to viable interior Korrath drift volume using local-observation navigation. Preserve crew, segregated data buffers, and minimum jump capability. Accept that retreat—not engagement victory—is the only rational outcome.

**Secondary Constraints:**

Maintain emissions discipline where possible. Do not merge primary and redundant sensor authority. Do not transmit on fleet bands. Do not assume hostile traffic is uniformly hostile without Park verification.

---



The lock tone stabilized—two tones, actually, primary and redundant disagreeing on harmonic but agreeing on intent.

Calder: "Halden. Probability."

"Seventy-one percent intercept window if we hold position through next closure cycle on primary. Redundant says forty-nine if we burn lateral now. Both bad. Lateral less bad."

Reyes, navigation: "Local chart shows segment four thermal null two-point-one hours drift at current whisper thrust. Fleet egress solutions are lies here. Interior vector is the only chart we trust."

Thessaly: "Walsh to damage-control liaison. Cole, relay congestion protocol—priority medical and nav only. Tanaka, sync with Reyes on color markers. Ortega, keep telling us when arrays disagree."

Calder heard the ship become one organism—not his heroics, their anticipations.

Contact two's illumination washed the forward scope in a pale strobing wash that passive baffles could not fully swallow. Mass profile on primary swelled into escort-class certainty—not a name, not a flag, a capability statement.

Koss's voice from engineering ring, flat and immediate: "Hull harmonic just shifted. That's not drift. That's targeting paint bleeding through Margaret's jacket."

Hale: "I can warm Margaret to defensive pulse in forty seconds. Need your authorization before I make us visible on purpose."

Calder looked at Halden's probability cone. Looked at Reyes's interior vector. Looked at the scope where followers had been geometry and were now intent.

"Warm Margaret. Defensive pulse only. Thessaly—execute lateral whisper burn on Reyes's null vector. Now."

"Aye."

The *Kestrel Veil* moved like a scout that had been built for exactly this kind of ugly—not fast, not proud, correct.

---


The first hostile action was not a beam. It was a denial.

Ortega: "Primary sweep collapsing—structured jamming on bands three through six. Redundant still sees clean space where primary sees wall."

Halden: "They're not trying to kill us yet. They're trying to pick which truth we die inside."

Park: "Sideband burst—fragmented. Not fleet header. Could be Ghost Fleet listener overlap. Could be decoy. Logging either way."

Calder: "Park—hostile or noise?"

"Both. The hostile part is the part that knew our buffer segregation keyword from the KR-14 boarding ping. That's not weather."

Calder felt the suppression fact Walsh had documented turn from background into targeting data. Someone had been listening long enough to learn how the *Veil* filed its sins.

Thessaly's burn completed. Lateral drift bit. Weapons lock quality dipped on Hale's panel—Margaret's first defensive pulse scattering return geometry the way Hale had promised in monosyllables and Halden had trusted without jokes.

Then contact one committed.

A narrow-band emission lanced through jamming weather—not a full weapons strike, a surgical cut along the port sensor mast housing. Ortega's primary array flickered, screamed in telemetry, and died to sixty-one percent functionality in one second.

Ortega did not panic. She panicked in certification language.

"Primary mast segment four offline. I still have redundant and shadow log. Truth just got slower."

Reyes: "I can navigate slower. I cannot navigate blind."

"You are not blind," Ortega said. "You are annoyed. Continue."

Calder almost smiled. He did not have the calories.

The followers did not hail. That mattered. A hail would have been a social fact—identity pretending to exist. Instead they offered capability: jamming as architecture, cuts as grammar, silence as confidence that the *Veil* would eventually run out of correct decisions.

Thessaly's whisper burn held course while Halden fed Hale closure rates in numbers stripped of metaphor. Walsh logged pressure door cycles. Okwelu logged Walsh. The ship's ordinary paperwork continued inside extraordinary violence—the scout-class proof that professionalism was not calm, it was structure under load.

---


The second cut was meant for Margaret.

It caught the emitter control bus instead—portside defensive rack, pre-position checklist still green on Halden's board from boarding-extraction discipline. Hale's panel went dark except for one analog fallback Halden had made him wire because Fleet procurement had once shipped a tactical subsystem that forgot how to fail gracefully.

Hale swore once, quietly, with the affection of a man addressing a tool that had betrayed a friend.

"Control bus is fried. Margaret's on manual stagger only. I can rebuild emitter sequencing from junction box if Brenner gives me live power and Koss lets me bypass the safety interlock on rack two."

Koss, without pause: "I heard my name and the word bypass. Explain before I kill you."

"Forty-second pulse windows. Manual close on my mark. If I miss the mark, Margaret cooks off and we learn what scout-class interior looks like from outside."

Koss: "Brenner. Live power. Junction box. You have thirty seconds of my attention."

Brenner's voice, already breathless: "On it. If this panel survives, I'm putting birthday candles on it."

"Don't," Hale said. "Margaret hates celebrations."

Halden leaned toward Hale's station without leaving tactical secondary. "When you're back—recommend timing."

Hale's hands moved in the wire-and-toggle language scouts learned when manuals assumed someone else would die first. "Stagger pulse on closure nadir. They expect continuous return during burn. Give them a hole instead."

Halden ran it once in the model. "That drops intercept probability eleven points on primary. Redundant says six. Ugly either way."

"Ugly is my specialty," Hale said.

Calder watched a petty officer second class rebuild weapons sequencing under fire while a lieutenant tactical officer asked his opinion like it mattered. That was the crew the anomaly weather had been raining on since KR-12. He delegated because delegating was the only honest command left.

"Hale—execute your suggestion on my mark. Halden—track both arrays. Do not merge."

"Aye," Hale said.

"Aye," Halden said.

The next closure cycle came. Hale punched manual stagger. Margaret breathed light in a rhythm the followers had not modeled. Lock quality fractured.

For six seconds the *Kestrel Veil* was not a target. It was a problem.

Contact two adjusted—too fast for passive drift, too precise for guesswork. Halden tracked the correction without cursing.

"They learn quick," he said.

Hale did not look up from the junction box. "Margaret learns quicker. She holds grudges."

"Noted," Halden said, and meant it.

---


Contact three appeared on redundant where primary saw nothing—then both arrays agreed it was real and closing from a vector Reyes's local chart called **stable dark** mouth, which was either a waypoint or a door and was now definitely an ambush geometry.

Thessaly: "Hull stress on ring three. Brenner—report."

"Cosmetic unless Koss says otherwise. Also Hale's junction box is holding. Barely. I'm naming the barely after myself."

"Don't," Koss said.

Sharma from environmental: "Thermal vent routing maxed. I'm using atmosphere exchange as misdirection—dumping warm bleed through decoy baffles on starboard. It's not much."

"It's ammunition," Halden said. "It's enough to confuse their second-pass targeting."

Calder heard Sharma's grandmother on Helion Prime in the word **ammunition**—weekly messages from a granddaughter spending CO₂ margin so the ship would not shine. Dark running had taught him Sharma's competence. Combat taught him its tactical grammar.

Walsh moved through the command deck perimeter with his watch notebook finally open—damage-control liaison, Thessaly's assignment, timestamps for Okwelu before Okwelu asked. Okwelu herself secured the auxiliary chair with med kit staged, seventeen degrees from vertical on purpose because someone on this crew always sat that way and she had stopped fighting it.

Tanaka called color markers to Reyes in the shorthand only they fully shared—thermal null, debris scatter, occluder sequence from KISU-7 core store. Reyes answered in fixes, not fleet coordinates.

"Segment four null in eighteen minutes at whisper. Then segment five if hull allows."

"Hull allows ugly," Koss said. "Hull does not allow stupid. I will tell you which you are choosing."

Cole at secondary relay: "Congestion spike. I'm dropping nonessential channels. Park—your sideband just tried to handshake with itself."

Park: "That's noise."

"That's *weird* noise."

"Still logging as noise until it shoots someone."

Cole's congestion management was its own battle—relay architecture never designed for jamming stacks and narrow-path stubs and hostile metadata fishing at once. She dropped galley entertainment bands first, then noncritical environmental chatter, then every duplicate handshake Park marked as weather. The relay breathed because she refused to let it drown.

"Priority lanes locked," Cole said. "Medical, nav, engineering. If anyone wants to send a birthday message to Brenner today, it waits."

Brenner, on live power: "Rude."

"Survival rude," Cole said.

The first real strike hit ring four aft— not a hull breach, a concussive tooling impact that shoved the *Veil* two degrees off Reyes's null approach and sent Walsh into a bulkhead hard enough to split his eyebrow.

Okwelu was on him before Calder finished turning.

"Concussion watch. Stay conscious for me, Walsh."

"Notebook," Walsh said.

Thessaly caught the notebook before it slid under a console. She handed it back without comment. Walsh closed it with blood on the cover and stayed on station because secondary monitoring did not pause for eyebrow geometry.

Calder: "Damage report."

Koss: "Ring four pressure door seven-C compromised. Sealed. Atmosphere stable in aggregate. Sharma—confirm."

"Aggregate stable. Medical bay priority holding. Berth feels angry. Not breached."

"Hull integrity?" Calder asked.

Koss listened—to the ship, not the panel. Sound before alarms. That was her doctrine.

"Eighty-four percent effective. We are older than we were an hour ago."

---


The followers tried to herd again—redundant array showing closure from segment five mouth while primary insisted segment four remained open. The same geometry Halden had logged during dark running, except now the herding carried teeth.

Park: "Captain—authenticated fragment on narrow path. Three words. Cut mid-syllable."

Calder's chest tightened. "Content."

"Not fleet. Not hostile fire. Listen-only stub I've been cycling since KR-14. Checksum valid on narrow path only. Text says—" Park read without embellishment: "'—hold interior—'"

Then silence.

Halden: "Trap bait."

Reyes: "Or someone inside Korrath still thinks interior is a vocabulary."

Calder looked at Park. Park looked at Cole. Cole's congestion log showed the fragment had arrived during jamming peak—not before, not after. Timing was a weapon.

"Hostile traffic masquerading as narrow path?" Calder asked.

"Partially," Park said. "The checksum is real. The truncation is hostile. Someone copied a true stub and weaponized the cut. I can separate them if you give me twenty seconds and Cole stops letting the relay breathe."

Cole: "Relay's not breathing. It's hyperventilating."

Calder: "Twenty seconds."

Park worked. The command deck held the kind of quiet that was not peace—problem-solving at combat tempo.

"Separated," Park said. "True stub remains: hold interior review language from suppression header family. Hostile overlay removed. Do not merge. Do not respond. Do not treat as permission."

Calder exhaled once. "Copy. Log to buffer seven chain only."

He had almost answered a voice that wanted them deeper. That was the enemy's partial win already—learning the *Veil* listened.

Park added, quieter: "The hostile overlay used our boarding callsign cadence. Not content. Cadence. Someone has been close enough to listen to how we breathe on comm."

Halden: "KR-14 shadow geometry. Platform extraction. They were there before we were."

Calder filed the fact without merging it into motive. Motive was luxury. Capability was calendar.

---


Reyes brought them into segment four thermal null on whisper thrust with primary sensor truth degraded and redundant screaming contacts that primary sometimes denied. Navigation by observation in anomaly weather—exactly the human competence Act II had been building toward.

Halden: "Intercept window reopening. Primary says contact one ranging for tag shot."

Calder: "Define tag."

"Low-yield emitter burst. Hull paint equivalent. They want persistence more than kill."

Thessaly: "If they tag us, fleet charts won't be the only thing that lies about where we've been."

Calder: "Hale—can Margaret break tag window?"

Hale, hands still on manual stagger: "Once. Maybe twice. Then Margaret needs a funeral."

Halden: "Do it once. Burn through null. Reyes—next fix."

Reyes: "Segment five occluder chain. Deeper. Interior. Fleet egress is dead behind us."

Calder heard the mission change in the sentence without anyone naming it. They were not holding at fringe. They were not returning to L-3 on Thessaly's abort solution. The followers and the jamming and the tag window had closed the back door by force.

"Execute," Calder said.

Margaret pulsed. Tag shot lanced past starboard baffles—close enough to heat plating, not close enough to bind. Then contact one tried again.

The second tag did not miss.

A dull thunk transmitted through ring three—the sound Koss had warned about, harmonic shift that was not drift. Ortega: "Persistent emission signature on hull port-aft. Active ping returning on bands we can't silence without killing nav."

Halden: "They tagged us."

Calder: "How bad?"

"For escape? Survivable. For anonymity? Dead."

Reyes slid the *Veil* into segment five occluder shadow—local chart marks Tanaka sequenced in colors, debris scatter from Okonkwo's store, thermal nulls fleet merge had never catalogued. The followers' closure geometry lagged one cycle behind, then two, as if superior mass did not instantly translate to superior chart literacy inside Korrath interior.

That was the escape—not winning. Losing slower than intercept.

Contact two fired once more—not at hull, at comm buffer hardware in ring two. Cole screamed—a professional sound, not pain.

"Partial buffer loss on relay archive. Segregated copies intact. They just ate our last clean outbound packet queue."

Park: "They recovered partial transmission metadata. Not content. Not yet."

"Yet," Calder repeated.

Thessaly: "Occluder chain ends in eleven minutes. After that we need jump prep or we need luck."

Koss: "Jump drive warm but not happy. Brenner and I can bring it online in one cycle if you accept draw spike."

Calder looked at Halden.

"Jump prep probability of survival versus continue drift?"

Halden did not joke. "Jump now with degraded sensors—sixty-three primary, fifty-one redundant. Drift deeper eleven minutes then jump—fifty-eight primary, sixty-two redundant. Both assume they don't get another tag window."

Reyes: "Interior jump solution ready on local chart. Exit variance ugly. Destination deeper Korrath. Not fringe. Not L-3."

Calder: "Thessaly."

"Recommend jump on Reyes's local solution. We cannot win a standfight. We can vanish badly."

"Calder," Halden said—rare use of his first name, not familiarity, urgency. "They're massing for intercept on primary. Redundant says they're already late. Arrays disagree. Pick the least bad."

Calder picked.

"Koss—jump online. Reyes—solution. Hale—cold Margaret after final pulse. Park—listen-only through jump. No fleet shout. Ever."

"Aye," from six stations at once.

---


The jump prep spike lit every emissions discipline they had spent eight hours building. Sharma swore in a dialect her grandmother would have scolded and rerouted bleed through every decoy baffle left intact. Hale ran Margaret cold with hands that shook once and then stopped because Halden was watching and Halden's respect was not given for steady hands alone.

Tanaka and Reyes merged local fixes in the only merge Calder still trusted—human observation over institutional chart.

"Solution locked," Reyes said. "Exit variance zero-point-zero-one-four parsecs. Local chart only. Fleet would call this insane."

"Fleet isn't here," Thessaly said.

The intercept tone returned—worse, harmonized across arrays for the first time all engagement. The followers had stopped disagreeing about whether to kill them and started agreeing about where.

Koss: "Drive online in five. Four. Three—"

The *Kestrel Veil* jumped wrong on purpose—the scout-class way, the Reyes way, the way you left when winning was not on the menu.

Light bent. Sound became memory. Calder's stomach became negotiable.

Then stillness.

Jump exit nausea was the body's complaint about geometry it had not voted for. Calder swallowed it. On a scout, you swallowed or you stepped aside for someone who would.

Ortega ran contact sweep twice because once was how people died in anomaly weather. Tanaka verified color markers against a star field that looked, to Calder's tired eyes, like every other star field and was not.

"Clear for now," Ortega said. "Define *for now*."

Halden: "Minimum eighteen minutes if their tag telemetry matches primary closure models. Longer if redundant lies again."

"Comforting," Ortega said.

"Accurate," Halden said.

---


Jump exit brought silence that hurt worse than jamming.

Ortega: "Primary mast segment four still offline. Redundant functional. Contacts—"

Pause.

"None on either array."

Halden: "Hold models. They may be late. They may be gone. Do not trust quiet."

Calder unclenched his hand from the command rail. "Damage roll call."

Koss: "Hull integrity eighty-one percent. Jump stress micro-fractures ring three. Manageable. Drive operational at reduced efficiency. Margaret needs a full rebuild. Control bus destroyed."

Hale: "Margaret sends her regards."

Nobody laughed. The line landed anyway—dry engineering sarcasm in the ash of action. Halden nodded once at Hale. That was respect on this deck.

Sharma: "Atmosphere aggregate stable. CO₂ margin five-point-four days. We spent Sharma currency."

"Worth it," Calder said.

Park: "No relay. No sideband. Narrow path silent. We are deeper than our last logged position by any fleet standard."

Reyes, navigation, voice flat: "We can't go back that way."

Calder looked at him. Reyes did not elaborate. He did not need to. Segment five occluder chain, tag signature on hull, partial comm metadata lost to hostile recovery, jump solution that only local observation trusted—all of it pointed one direction.

Interior.

Okwelu moved Walsh to medical for eyebrow suture and concussion protocol. Tanaka reported nav station shock bruising—minor, logged. Brenner's left glove was charred at the palm from live-power work; he refused med until Koss ordered it in the voice that ended arguments.

Calder left the command deck for ring four damage control because captains who only watched became passengers, and because his legs needed to remember the ship was physical.

In corridor C, junction seven-C sealed behind a temp plate Brenner had bolted with one hand while the other still smelled like ozone. A damage-control tech Calder almost recognized held a crate of sealant foam and stepped aside without saluting—Fleet habit long dead on this ship.

"Need the hatch clear, Captain."

"Which hatch?"

"Seven-C auxiliary. Foam sets in four minutes."

Calder moved. The tech moved with him—efficient, unremarkable, the sort of presence passageways produced under load.

"Thanks," Calder said.

"Sure," the tech said.

Calder continued to medical summary without remembering the face. Later—if later came—he would not be able to place the exchange in any watch log. Tessa Morales, if she had been aboard, might have noticed the foam lot number in a maintenance string nobody would merge. She was not aboard. Nobody else would align.

The tech was already gone from the corridor when Calder looked back.

In medical bay, Okwelu had Walsh under suture light and Tanaka in the adjacent bay seat for contusion wrap. The eyebrow wound was shallow. The concussion watch was not.

"Sit," Okwelu told Calder when he entered.

"I am not your patient."

"You are everyone's patient. Sit anyway."
